gall is the abnormal growth (gall) produced by parasitic aphids (insect) of Melaphis chinensis on the leaf or stems of Rhus chinensis (Chinese sumac), Rhus potaninii (potanin sumac) or Rhus punjabensis (red-punjab sumac), a deciduous dungarunga of the family Anacardiaceae. The gall aphid deposits a larva in leaves and stems of these trees and makes them produce the gall that looks like a hard and round excretions [1-2]. Galls (including Chinese gall) are rich in resins, tannic acid, and gallic acid [3-4]. Recent study shows the Chinese gall might exert anti-diabetic effect by suppressing carbohydrate absorption from intestine, and thereby reducing the postprandial increase of blood glucose [5]. Chinese gall will be considered as a NHP, under Schedule 1, item 1, Priority 4 (Plant material).

Hussien/HC-SC/GC/CA wrote on 2009-03-27 04:17:21 PM:
Echium oil is extracted from seeds of the Echium plantagineum plant. It contains Palmitic, Oleic, Stearic, Linoleic, α-Linolenic, γ-Linolenic, and Stearidonic acids (Frank Gunstone 2007). Echium oil increases plasma and neutrophil long-chain (n-3) fatty acids and lowers serum triacylglycerols in hypertriglyceridemic humans (Surette, Edens et al. 2004). Recent studies have shown that echium oil and other oils containing stearidonic acid as well as botanical oil combinations (such as echium and borage oils) hold great promise for modulating inflammatory diseases (Chilton, Rudel et al. 2008). Echium oil will be considered as a NHP, under Schedule 1, item 2, Priority 4 (Extract).

References: 
Chilton, F. H., L. L. Rudel, et al. (2008). "Mechanisms by which botanical lipids affect inflammatory disorders." Amer Soc Clinical Nutrition 87(2): 498S-503S. 
Frank Gunstone, J. H., Albert Dijkstra (2007). The Lipid Handbook, CRC Press. 
Surette, M. E., M. Edens, et al. (2004). "Dietary echium oil increases plasma and neutrophil long-chain (n-3) fatty acids and lowers serum triacylglycerols in hypertriglyceridemic humans." an interesting classification of Bee Venom:
Bee venom (Apitoxin) (US-NLM 2009) is a complex mixture of vasoactive amines (dopamine, histamine, norepinephrine), enzymes (phospholipase A 2 , hyaluronidase, acid phosphatase, lysophospholipase, α-d-glucosidase), and toxic peptides (melittin, apamin, protease inhibitor, secapin, peptide 401) (Barceloux 2008). Bee venom showed to have anti-arthritis, anti-cancer, and pain release (anti-nociceptive) effect (Son, Lee et al. 2007). Bee venom will be considered as a NHP, under schedule 1, item 1, Priority 3 (Non-human animal material).
References: 
Barceloux, D. G. (2008). Medical Toxicology of Natural Substances: Foods, Fungi, Medicinal Herbs, Plants, and Venomous Animals, John Wiley and Sons. 
Son, D. J., J. W. Lee, et al. (2007). "Therapeutic application of anti-arthritis, pain-releasing, and anti-cancer effects of bee venom and its constituent compounds." Pharmacology & Therapeutics 115(2): 246-270. 
US-NLM. (2009). oil is used as a source of polyunsaturated fatty acids, mainly EPA and DHA. (Lin 1995). The EPA and DHA in squid oil are distributed along the sn-2 position of the oil triglyceride (Ikeda 1998). Squid oil is very oxygen-sensitive and that has limited its use in food: however microencapsulation is commonly used to provide additional stabilisation to squid oil (Lin 1995).
Squid oil will be considered as an NHP, under item 2 (extract) of Schedule 1 of the NHP regulations.

References: 
Lin C, Lin S, Hwang L. 1995. Microencapsulation of squid oil with hydrophylic macromolecules for oxidative and thermal oxidation, Journal of Food Science. 60(1): 36-39

Merci
Martin Charron";s:29:"field_quantity_restriction_on";s:0:"";s:17:"field_rationale_e";s:96:"Classified as an NHP under Schedule 1 item 1 (non-human animal material) of the NHP Regulations.";s:17:"field_rationale_f";s:158:"Consideré comme PSN sous Annexe 1, article 1, matière animale autre qu'une matière provenant de l'humain, du Règlement sur les produits de santé naturels";s:24:"field_restriction_desc_e";s:0:"";s:24:"field_restriction_desc_f";s:0:"";s:17:"field_upper_limit";s:0:"";s:10:"field_date";s:0:"";s:16:"field_legacy_nid";s:5:"83747";s:14:"field_workflow";s:9:"Published";s:30:"field_approved_ingredient_name";s:16:"Dried egg powder";s:20:"field_ingredient_cat";s:3:"ABS";s:25:"field_nhpd_classification";s:6:"NHANML";s:10:"field_unit";s:0:"";s:20:"field_limitation_ref";s:0:"";s:9:"field_ref";s:4:"NHPD";s:15:"field_other_ref";s:0:"";s:5:"title";s:20:"Dried egg powder_ABS";s:3:"nid";s:5:"88718";s:7:"changed";s:10:"2013-10-16";s:14:"workflow_state";s:9:"Published";}i:88719;a:25:{s:4:"body";s:0:"";s:21:"field_ingredient_role";s:2:"MI";s:13:"field_its_ids";s:9:"1953;3027";s:17:"field_lower_limit";s:0:"";s:19:"field_old_rationale";s:2546:"Proper Name(s): Kombucha (NMCD 2010, Fundukian 2009)
Common Name(s): Kombucha (NMCD 2010, Fundukian 2009)

Kombucha is a beverage produced by fermenting sugared tea (e.g. sucrose and/or glucose in black and/or green tea) with a symbiosis of bacteria (e.g. Acetobacter xylinum, Acetobacter xylinoides, and/or Bacterium gluconicum) and yeast strains (e.g. Schizosaccharomyces pombe, Saccharomycodes ludwigii, Saccharomyces cerevisiae, and/or Fangus japonicus) (Fundukian 2009, Sreeramulu et al. 2000). The resulting broth is characterized by a floating cellulosic pellicle layer, where the cell mass of bacteria and yeasts is attached, on a sour liquid, containing acetic acid, ethanol, and gluconic acid as the major constituents, as well as substances like lactic acid, glucuronic acid, phenolic acid, B vitamins and enzymes as minor constituents (Chu and Chen 2006). Kombucha proved to exert antimicrobial activities against a wide range of bacteria, such as Escherichia coli, Shigella sonnei, Salmonella typhimurium, Salmonella enteritidis, and Campylobacter jejuni, even at neutral pH and after thermal denaturation (Sreeramulu et al. 2000). Apart from the organic acids, such as the ones mentioned above, the tea polyphenols (e.g. ()-epicatechin, ()-epicatechin gallate, ()-epigallocatechin, ()-epigallocatechin gallate, thearubigen, and theaflavin] are also believed to be active constituents present in kombucha tea, possessing a number of beneficial effects (Jayabalan et al. 2007).

Based on the above, Kombucha meets the substance aspect of the Natural Health Product (NHP) definition, under Schedule 1, item 2 (extract) of the NHP Regulations (Government of Canada 2010).

References:

It contains lactic acid bacteria (Lactobacillus, Lactococcus, Leuconostoc), sometimes acetic acid bacteria, lactose-fermenting yeasts (Kluyveromyces lactis, Kluyveromyces marxianus and Torula kefir), and non-lactose-fermenting yeasts (Sacchoromyces cerevisiae). The yeasts are important in kefir fermentation, as they produce carbon dioxide and hence the characteristic effervescence [Magalhaes et al. 2010]. Experimental results in a mouse model have shown that some of the low molecular weight peptides produced during fermentation interact with the gut wall cells and stimulate the immune system, which may help protect against disease and infection. The polysaccharide kefiran, composed of repeating units of branched hexa- and heptasaccharides of mainly glucose and galactose [Guzel-Seydim et al. 2005], is also thought to assist the immune system [AAFC 2006]. Kefir will be considered a NHP under Schedule 1, item 1, non-human animal material, of the Natural Health Products Regulations.

The FDA regulates such products as drugs when there are therapeutic claims.  If a sponsor wished to market a product containing colostrum with a therapeutic claim, the product would be classified as a drug (biologic or NHP). Currently products containing colostrum cannot be classified as biologics because it (colostrum) is not listed in Schedule D. Furthermore, those products that claim to support the immune system are not considered to be biologics as they do not meet the definition of “immunizing agents” as listed on Schedule D.  Claims proven to meet the definition of “immunizing agents” would fall under Schedule D and any unproven claims would be in violation of the Act and Regulations.

Immunoglobulins are non-NHPs because they are listed in Schedule D of the Food and Drugs Act as follows: Immunizing agents. As such, total immunoglobulins are excluded from the definition of a Natural Health Product (NHP). Natural health products like Bovine colostrum and Goat milk colostrum, for example, cannot be standardised to either total or specific horn: Saiga tatarica is an antelope from genus Saiga which is concentrated in three main areas within central Asia: Mongolia, Kazakhstan, and Kalmykia (Michigan 2009). The horns have some value as traditional Chinese medicine (TCM) and for that reason Saiga are now endangered by poaching. Based on the TCM, antelope horn extinguishes wind, calms Liver, drains heat, reduces toxin, and brightens eyes (Liu, Tseng et al. 2005). An extract of antelope horn showed mild caspase-inhibitory activity (activation of caspases is a critical step in neuronal apoptosis/programmed cell death) (Fattorusso, Frutos et al. 2006). Antelope horn will be considered as a NHP, under schedule 1, item 1, Priority 3 (Non-human animal material).

References:
Fattorusso, R., S. Frutos, et al. (2006). "Traditional Chinese medicines with caspase-inhibitory activity." Phytomedicine 13(1-2): 16-22.
Liu, C., A. Tseng, et al. (2005). Chinese Herbal Medicine- Modern Applications of Traditional Formulas, CRC Press.
Michigan, U. (2009). is a genus of microalgae (Class: Phycomycota, algae-like fungi) from the family Thraustochytriaceae (ITIS 2010, GBIF 2010). Schizochytrium  and its oil are rich sources of omega-3 fatty acids, notably DHA (Doughman et al. 2007).
Based on the above, Schizochytrium and its oil meet the substance aspect of the Natural Health Product (NHP) definition, under Schedule 1, item 1 (alga) or 2 (extract), of the NHP Regulations (Government of Canada 2010).

the revised response that is already revised and approved by Robin:

Red yeast rice refers to the product of fermentation of rice with various strains of the yeast Monascus purpureus. Red yeast rice has been used for centuries in China in foods and in medicines. It was introduced in the U.S. during the latter half of the 1990s as a dietary supplement for the promotion of healthy serum lipid levels. Since red yeast rice contains the HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor lovastatin that is listed on the Schedule F to the Food and Drug Regulations without qualifiers, Health Canada has proposed an amendment to Schedule F to set a limit for lovastatin below which it would be acceptable in an NHP. Until such time as lovastatin's Schedule F listing is amended with qualifiers and published in Canada Gazette II and thus becomes official, Red yeast rice must be lovastatin-free to meet the definition of an NHP as per Schedule 1, item 1 (Priority 4; plant material) of the NHP Regulations.";s:29:"field_quantity_restriction_on";s:0:"";s:17:"field_rationale_e";s:449:"Classified as an NHP under Schedule 1 item 1 (plant or plant material) of the NHP Regulations. Erigerontis";s:20:"field_ingredient_cat";s:3:"AHS";s:25:"field_nhpd_classification";s:5:"PLANT";s:20:"field_route_of_admin";s:6:"TOPICL";s:10:"field_unit";s:0:"";s:20:"field_limitation_ref";s:2:"CP";s:9:"field_ref";s:4:"NHPD";s:15:"field_other_ref";s:0:"";s:5:"title";s:21:"Herba Erigerontis_AHS";s:3:"nid";s:5:"95157";s:7:"changed";s:10:"2013-10-16";s:14:"workflow_state";s:9:"Published";}i:95158;a:26:{s:4:"body";s:0:"";s:21:"field_ingredient_role";s:2:"MI";s:13:"field_its_ids";s:4:"3027";s:17:"field_lower_limit";s:0:"";s:19:"field_old_rationale";s:2081:"Kefir is an effervescent fermented dairy product made by inoculating cow, goat or sheep milk with kefir grains, irregular granules varying in size from 3 to 35 mm in diameter. It contains lactic acid bacteria (Lactobacillus, Lactococcus, Leuconostoc), sometimes acetic acid bacteria, lactose-fermenting yeasts (Kluyveromyces lactis, Kluyveromyces marxianus and Torula kefir), and non-lactose-fermenting yeasts (Sacchoromyces cerevisiae). These live microorganisms, along with casein and complex sugars, exist in a matrix of polysaccharides called kefiran. The yeasts are important in kefir fermentation, as they produce carbon dioxide and hence the characteristic effervescence [Magalhaes et al. 2010]. Experimental results in a mouse model have shown that some of the low molecular weight peptides produced during fermentation interact with the gut wall cells and stimulate the immune system, which may help protect against disease and infection. The polysaccharide kefiran, composed of repeating units of branched hexa- and heptasaccharides of mainly glucose and galactose [Guzel-Seydim et al. 2005], is also thought to assist the immune system [AAFC 2006]. Kefir will be considered a NHP under Schedule 1, item 1, non-human animal material, of the Natural Health Products Regulations. References AAFC 2006.
